O, children, now that I have completed this book, which is the last of the science of medicine, by dedicating it to its end and by the explanations and declarations within it, it is clear to me that I complete for you here this treatise, which deals with surgical operations.
Since the operation of surgery is widespread in our region and in our times, yet it remains almost entirely private, despite its clear importance and utility; and only some small descriptions are found in the works of the Ancients, which correct many errors and hesitations within them, the understanding of it has been obstructed and its force and art have been diminished.
And it is clear to me that I should vivify that order of this treatise according to the way of explanation and declaration and abbreviation; and for it to come in the forms of the tools of cautery and other instruments of the work, through the addition of declaration and preparation, which cause is necessary.
More the reason why a good craftsman does not touch his hand well in this our time is because the art of medicine is long and it covers the actor before this to be exercised in the science of anatomy which Gualia knew among that he was acquainted with the youth of the members and their forms, and the conjunction and separation of them; and had knowledge of the bones, the nerves, and the ligaments, and the number of those, and their qualities; and of the veins pulsating and the quiet ones, and the places of the effusion of them.
And for this Hippocrates says that in his name is written, more in the little work, and especially in the art of the hand.
More however we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for who does not know what we said about anatomy, will not be free from error through which many men will die, as I have seen too many of those, for some are formed in this science and fall into that other science and its experiments.
For you saw a foolish and inexperienced doctor who incurred an apostematia escrophilosa in the neck of a woman, and injured some arteries so that great blood flowed until the woman died amidst much suffering.
And there was another doctor who almost performed an extraction of a stone from a man who had been suffering from his condition, and the stone was large, and came to him, and he dried that stone with a piece of bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I have already called for the extraction of that stone, and I saw that due to the size of the stone and the position of the patient over whom it was located, he would die.
And there was another doctor who took surgical fees from a duke of our land, and he arrived at a blacksmith named Negre who had a fracture in the thigh; and because of the doctor's ignorance, he dried the fracture, over the wound, with pillows and splints with strong structure, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then the dislocation according to his desires, and then the reattachment, and then the drying for several days and commanded that we release the bandage; and thus remained until the thigh and foot became well, and then proceeded to recovery.
And I was called to him, and prepared to untie the ligament, and peace and an attempt to alleviate his pains was achieved.
But however, since corruption was confirmed in the member, I could not prevent it, and did not cease to walk the corruption through the member while the illness persisted.
And there was another doctor who incised an malignant abscess, and it ulcerated for several days, during which its malignity increased greatly in the lord of the area.
The cause is that the pain is purely due to melancholic humors, and does not affect the entire body, but rather affects a member that suffers from being attacked by all.
And for this reason, my son, it is necessary that you devise the operation in two divisions: one where the operation is associated with health, and another where the work is perilous in its major preparations.
And I have already existed here in all parts of this book where the operation is associated with error and fear; therefore, it is necessary that you keep this and leave the other, so that the leaves do not find a way to speak ill of you.
And therefore take your weapons with diligence and protection of God, and your patients with felicity and firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God seeking salvation and praiseworthy success, and lay aside the terrible diseases difficult to cure, and remain with your weapons against what frightens you, so that sitting in your faith and in your name you are not overcome: for more remains to your glory, and greater still in this world and beyond in your sanctity.
Gum disease is observed in some cases: Do not treat disease improperly, so that bad doctors are not named.
And I have already devoted this livre according to three chapters.
Begin the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered of the head concerning the feet, in the forms of the instruments and in the tools of the cautery, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on drying and perforation, and on cups, and on plaques, and on the extraction of arrows and similar, all categorized and organized by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and on the healing of tortoises and similar conditions; a chapter organized by the head between the feet, and by the forms of the instruments.
I will now describe the quality of the metals used for the cautery and its components, and in which condition a cautery should not be used.
Therefore, I say that the discourse on the quality of the cautery metal and its component is long and prolix, subtle by science, and secretly hidden; and much has been spoken about it by wise men, but they are in disagreement. For me, a little from their sermons is enough to avoid lengthy discussions.
And therefore it is said that a cautery is applied to any entire composition universally which is either hot or of a hot nature, except for two compositions, which are gout compositions.
In the gouty and dry composition of matter, it desiccates; for some said that a cautery is beneficial for that, while others said the opposite, that a cautery is not convenient in the malady which is due to quality and dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that such a malady, being dry and of matter, be healed by what is hot and dry.
And he who says the opposite asserts that by cautery one is healed in all gouty and dry maladies that come into men's bodies, for when you compare the body to its humidity to the composition of fire, you find the body to be hot.
And I say according to the speech of that person, who has already experienced this several times and discovered it.
But I do not know if it comes to that which is already experienced and exercised in the capital of cautery by recent exercises, and has known the diversity of the composition of people and the disposition of maladies in their members, and the causes of those, and the accidents of them, and the space of time of those.
In other complications, there is no need to fear, and especially in the cold and damp diseases, for all doctors agree on those, and are not divided in using cautery in them.
Know, sons, that the secret of curing by cautery with fire and its good effect is based on the burning medical cautery, for fire is simple, and its action reaches only the member being cauterized and causes no great harm to other members.
But the action of the burning medical cautery reaches that part of the member which it touches, and strongly brings to the member pain of difficult healing and strongly sustains it.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does nothing but superfluous consume. We already know this from experience and long service in studying the art about the true causes; and for this reason, I excuse myself for the length of my discourse. If it were not that this is not written freely in this pause, and that you secretly keep the qualities of fire's operation on bodies and its expulsion by which you expel diseases, this discourse would be philosophical and demonstrative, suitable for your ears.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time at which it is convenient to perform a cautery and when the best season is spring.
And I say that a cautery is convenient at all times, because the damage from the accidental season is submerged in the youth of what is burned by the cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, strong and fistulas, which do not suffer delay because it is feared of those that come malice, which is greater than small damage entering through time.
And this is not done on your feet, sons, for they do not consider it except by the common people and the foolish doctors, since for that illness that is cured by cautery it is not convenient at all times, and they pause that consequence.
And the reason is not like this they consider it, for a cautery is not but in place of a medicine which alters the composition and dries the humors which are the cause of the wind's pains.
Yet, however, an excess cautery over medicine for the swiftness of its virtue and power of its operation and greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that his illness returns at some point in time, according to the complexity of the illness and the location of his illness, and its virtue, and what is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and what is obtained by him in his acquisition through food and semblances of those causes; but the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member with few superfluities and humidities, just as cautery for toothache due to pain and similar to that, it is possible that that pain does not return in it, more so when it is done in small amounts.
And more commonly said is that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a layman's saying, not what we understand by it, for I believe that it is not a cure that benefits either medicine or any other cause except cautery.
And the reason is the opposite; and I understand that cautery is the last remedy of medicine only when one administers the methods of cure for some illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and near the end of the cause, one administers cautery, and it benefits then. And here from this point cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to understanding which I understand of the vulgar and too many of the foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that a golden cautery is better than an iron one, and they did not say this because of the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place for cautery does not burn, since it is not absolutely heated, for I have already tried this and tested that it works only in some bodies and not in others. A golden cautery is good and better than iron as they say; but when you make a cautery with fire of gold, it is not up to you to declare, for it ignites according to the quantity you want due to the hardness of the gold, and prepares to receive heat; and if you adjust over the ignition, the gold melts by the fire, and some sheets remain in the fire for the craftsman to cut through the occupation of that.
And for this, you should make the cautery of iron lighter and more precisely to the right position during the operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on the cautery according to other chapters, and I have ordered those of the thigh between the legs, so that I delay the requesting party what he wants from him.
The first chapter.
This cautery addresses the cold and humidity over the servel accident of which there are two causes, which cause soda and much traumatic flux, called corissa, from the head to the parts of the hollows and of the ears flowing, and much sleeping, and toothache, and colic, and universally all illness that comes through coldness, thus as paralytically, soda, and apoplexy, and similar signs in those illnesses.
The form of this cautery is as follows: first, I command the patient to be evacuated with a laxative medicine for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, the age of the patient, and his custom.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and place that razor between your hands on a sloping bench so that he places his hands on his own foot.
And then place the razor from your palm against his cheek between his fingers. More importantly, since the correct location has been determined, mark that location with a probe, and then heat the olive cautery of which this is the form:
And then place that cautery over the marked location with a probe, in such a way that it descends gradually, and you rotate it.
And then lift your hand lightly and look at the location. If you see any part of the bone being revealed, as much as a grain of barley, then raise your hand. If not, return your hand to that iron, or another one if it is cooled, until what I have said can be seen on the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it over the location and leave it there for three days.
Then place your hand over the inguinal area where the fire scar is destroyed, and leave that hand on the inside. Then apply a tetra-pharmaceutical salve to the area until it is healed.
And we have already said that when the wound is opened wide, it heals better, because it is healthier and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the ankle should be cauterized inside the bone, and that the cautery should remain until part of the bone is burned.
Then scrape away what is burned from the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that the cautery should reach the marrow, pressing strongly into the bone until it cools, and this is the method of Alphécat, petit. And they believe that from that location the vapors of the head expire.
And keep the wound open for a long time, and then treat it until it is healed and consolidated.
But these two types of cautery I have not seen in all my days, except in some men according to a fear-based method; leave that one, I advise you, it is better for health when possible; for the head is heated by the solution of continuity of itself as we have testified in other members, and especially when the head of the patient is of cold nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, I advise you; use that one and do so for health.

which is cylindrical, as it forms like a cane or a bird's feather quill, from one end of which the cautery is made.

Chapter XXV.
From the roots to the extremities of the nose.
The roots are examined from the extremities of the nose, and are enlarged, fattened, and observed over several days as the swelling is noted.

And if the abscess is due to a blow and not dark in color, and examination is possible throughout that area, then drain the illness and examine it without fear, and treat the area with what dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
For the suturing of the nose and lips when the continuity is dissolved by wound or resemblance.
Know that when there is a solution of continuity in one of these cartilages, you should not place much trust in that operation but rather in several skilled individuals.

Then remove it and change the plaster, allowing it to remain until the threads dissolve by themselves. Finally, continue to treat with ointment until it is healed.
The method of suturing is that you add the solution of continuity or use needles as we have recommended in abdominal suture, or add the solution of continuity with thread as you are made to know.

Chapter XXVIII.
For the incision of flesh embedded in the gums.

Capitol.xl.
In which the main discourse on boils and their sections is found.
Boils are special kinds of swellings, occurring in various ways, as remembered, for each one in this treatise.
And they are diverse in their perforation and section through two methods. The one method is according to the nature of the boils themselves; and by this, we grasp the humors.
And the second method is near the places where bodily accidents occur. For a boil that occurs on the head is different from a boil that occurs in the buttocks; and a boil that occurs in a fleshly area is different from a boil that occurs at a joint; and each has its own judgment through operation.
And for abscesses some of them should be allowed to drain naturally rather than punctured until the healing process matures in those or their complement.
And for other abscesses, some of them should be drained when they are ripe, and not matured according to their complement. Thus, for abscesses that are near joints, the nature of such an abscess is prolonged into the area where you can see it in the circuit of the joint; or if the joint substance is corrupted by the decay of ligaments or nerves of that joint, it may cause contraction of that limb; or it is near a main member, and if you delay the puncturing of it until it matures, it will affect the main member; or it is near the anus, therefore drain what is not ripe there; and do not drain it raw due to poor digestion, but one near the anus, so that it does not cause prolapse and extend inside the anal parts, forming a fistula, or done in a term where it has not been healed. And you must know the time for puncturing the abscess, and this is when it is ripe.
And know that it is matured when there is a decrease in pain and fever, and the reduction of swelling and pulse, and the subsidence of the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And know that when you have identified the puncture point below the location of the abscess and discharge coming from the wound, proceed.
And the puncture should be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the thigh or foot, and at the location of the lacertae, the cords of the nerves, the arches; and, finally, in all locations where there is no reflection; for in those that are reflective, use puncture according to that member.
When the abscess is in a fleshly location, it is better to delay the puncture until the digestion of the pus is confirmed according to custom; as we have said. For if you puncture before this, you will prolong the course of the disease and increase its tenacity, as well as deepen the lesion.
More abscesses should be punctured according to the width of the flesh or body if necessary, or according to that from which the limb derives.
And ensure that you administer a small puncture in large abscesses, and a large and wide or multiple sections according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And in other cases of abscesses, ensure that the string is closed and tied when it is done so as if it were a bandage for wounds like those from dubelis and buboes.
And of those that are incised with a section having three angles; and in which it is incised as the shape of a mirtle leaf, as with abscesses of anglers.
And of these, there are those in which a round section and a lunar section are administered, and similar to those.
More for those where none is done so, as with superficial abscesses of lions, ensure that you puncture them with a superficial puncture, or flat one only.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected healthy pus, you should puncture it and not hesitate to extract all the foul matter in that hour; having extracted one part, then raise the abscess on another day, and extracting a larger part, and repeatedly do this until it is completely drained.
And especially if the patient is a man of virile virtue, or a woman in labor, or a small child, or an old person in decline, because the animal spirit resolves into the exit of the healthy fluid, and greatly alleviates the illness, and you may openly do this.
Take great care with this chapter, and when you puncture the abscess in which you are making the incision, ensure that you cleanse the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small, or a flat section, then administer the simples of either li or coto antic; if the abscess is large, and the cracks of the incision are deep, then you should place your hand in each part until they heal together.
And if in the abscess you have divided a part of the cord or have concave it in the circuit, then you should close it with old linen or thread made of li fabric, as when you have drawn out bandages or roots, to retain their moisture; strengthen it on the third day; and proceed with it and cure it as what is appropriate from ointments until it is healed.
But if you happen to have a flow of blood during the time of your operation, when you administer water mixed and wine in which soak a cloth, then place that over the location of the blood flow many times.
But if the flow of blood persists, then you must administer treatment and cure using powders which I have recommended in several places of this our book and division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is near many nerves, then you must infuse powders in wine and oil with a cautery, and place those over the locations.
And if it is in the summer, and the locations are fleshy, then you must place powders soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, all of which should be infused together by the third day, as we have said, and then you must incise the abscess, cleanse it, and administer to it what is appropriate for it to receive until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary concerning the knowledge of the cure of abscesses generally, but according to the way of distinction, I have already said all about the abscess in how the cure of it is conducted specifically by chapters.

Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the scrotal skin.
Spots on the scrotal skin in some men cause the flesh between them to soften, and it is suspected that this is due to such spots.
Then it is necessary for the one who is consulted in the treatment of this to make a incision according to his skill, and completely remove the scrotal skin that has softened over the natural scrotal skin; and then he should suture the two edges together.
And if you wish, make three or four initial incisions in the excess soft scrotal skin and perform a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and afterwards, treat for the other wounds until they heal and the stitches fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
Of castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but by cause it is related to me that I remember it in this treatise; and the memory of it is not necessary for two reasons, of which one is that when asked about it as a doctor, one should know the cure for whom it happens; and the other reason is that we may sometimes have need of castration for our young animals such as mules, oxen, pigs, and similar to beasts.
And thus it is said that castration is performed in two ways: either by incision or by making an incision and sectioning.
But in that which is performed by incision, the way of the operation is that we make the beast urinate in cold water while its genitals are soft and relaxed and hanging. And then take those parts in your hand while they are relaxed and no marks appear upon touch.
In castration performed through a crack and section, ensure that you have the animal and press the cord of the testicles with your left hand.
Then lift the suspensory ligament, and incise over each groove between two processes.
Then incise those glands that you have excoriated. And do not incise any of those on the scrotum for any cause, but incise the thin one which is over the veins.
And this method of castration is better which is performed by traction, for traction firmly leaves no cause of the testicles since the animal desires copulation.
Then cure the wound while it is healing.

And in the second kind for men from which urine flows which is in the root of the testicles, neither a cure nor an operation is done there.

Capitol.lix.
On the healing of alratica.
Alratica is when the womb of the woman is perforated, or is slightly torn. And it can be natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to ill-health which has preceded, and that is caused by flesh not fitting well or by a thin or thickened membrane.
It is caused in the region of the uterus, or in its sides, or in the upper part thereof, or in the lower part thereof, and you prohibit coitus and conception and childbirth, and strongly you prohibit menstruation.
The knowledge of that is made by looking at the midwife, if the obstruction is apparent, carefully; more so if it is not apparent, then you should feel it either through the thighs or through the abdomen.
More so if the obstruction is due to a thin membrane, and is close to the two lips, then you lift and separate that area.
And you place powders on the two lips, and then place your fingers indicating both hands around them.
And the woman is on her back, her thighs extended, and then widely separate the two lips until that thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.

And sometimes accidents occur in the natural abundance of other tissues, then you must incise according to this method. If this is due to an abscessed boil, for the abscessed boil which is in the greater part do not make any counter-irritation in any manner.

For the treatment of fistulas that discharge pus.
The fistulas that discharge low pus are swollen and large, which occur close to the anus outside or in the space of one side.
And it is a fistula or abscess.
And when that swelling is old and flows from watery white or subtle pus, sometimes some of these fistulas penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known as those arising from them due to the outflow and wind condition; sometimes they involve the patient's outflow, and a strong discharge is characteristic of those caused by worms.
And of these fistulas, sometimes they penetrate into the space between the bladder and the meat of the groin; and of those that penetrate between the thighs and between the scrotum.
And to distinguish between a penetrating and non-penetrating fistula, insert your finger into the anus and gently probe with a thin stick of reed or iron in the fistula when there is no pus discharge.
If there is hardness, then insert a thin rod of lead or horsehair between the layers where you feel the hard part.
And if you do not feel that hardness at all, nor any cause of obstruction due to feces, worms, or wind, as we have said, then you should know it is non-penetrant, and proceed with the treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrant into the bladder or into the meat of the penis, then the sign of this is either obstruction of urine from that side and prohibition to it, so that the area treated by medicine becomes consolidated.
And if it is penetrant into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the circuit, then the sign of this is that the hardness permeates here if there is no hardness in the bone, and pain in the circuit of the area; and that pus constantly flows from it; and that it does not respond to treatment, nor is it consolidated by ointment.
And in all these penetrant fistulas, complete cure is not always achieved, and the treatment of those is hard work and the diligence of the medical leaves upon which they operate.
In that which is not penetrating and not ancient, heal with iron as I will recount to you later, and what is already there and prepared.
And it is that the patient lies on his side with his knees bent towards his chest, the thighs inclined towards the abdomen of the patient, and then insert a lead tube or an iron one if it is in a tortuous fistula, into which you can feel the location where the tent passes.
And if the patient feels pain around the throat, then you must insert your finger into the throat.

For the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess when it is wrapped and allowed to become old, and forms a ulcer, and does not heal completely, continues to discharge pus constantly, then in any part of the body, it is universally named a fistula and we call it an abscess.
And according to truth, a fistula is a furred, hard, white node at which there is no pain and which is cavitated just like an abscess; therefore, it is called that same ulcer, i.e., a pain.
And in that ulcer the moisture gathers; and if the humidity is strong, it is absorbed within a few hours. And in some cases, this moisture is thick, small, and fine.

From the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to the species, and according to the places of the body in which they occur, for their diversity is that some have large points, small, concave, and silent, being solid; and others have three angles, and those to which belong tongues; and those to which belong feathers.
And of the signs of fever, when the arrow hits it and pierces through the bone, and is situated above the brain, which is on top of the skull, for it happens by intense heat, scotoma, vertigo, redness in the palms, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of understanding, and vomiting of bile, and the body becomes weak, speech is cut off, and voice destroyed, and situated at the location of the wound a white moisture resembling dust, and its appearance similar to the humidity of flesh.
And if you see these signs in this manner, then abstain from the cure and extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
And the signs of the arrow when stuck in the heart are that it is close to the left nipple, and one feels a hard lump there, which is not visible; and the strong movement of the arrow seems like the pulse, and the flow of the black and foul blood, and the coldness of the extremities, cyanosis, and syncope; and from this you know that death descends upon him completely.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are spitting of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are swollen, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes great sighs or demands air violently.
And if the arrow is stuck in the membrane which is in the foot, and the pain at that time is near the small bones, then it causes a great and violent pain and breathing difficulties, and not all the members' muscles are affected.
And if you touch the arrow in the stomach, then strongly press the wound with a piece of undigested food.
And if you touch the arrow in the abdomen, and it is stuck there, place a piece of the torn skin, or intestine, over the wound, or do not hope for its recovery through the removal of the arrow.
And if the arrow touches the bladder, and it penetrates through urine, and any process from it affects the parts outside, then strong pain over the wound indicates that it is fatal.
Moreover, the other limbs such as the face, neck, shoulder, arm, auxiliary muscles, spinal bones, furcula, costal cavity, and their counterparts of the limbs are also saved according to major dispositions when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a venomous arrow.
And I will recount to you what I have seen about the nature of these arrows, so that you may understand its significance for treatment.
The cause is that in a man an arrow struck the lacrimal of his eye in the root of his nose, and I extracted it from one side, left his ear gray, and he recovered, and was not damaged in his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which struck him in his eye gray, left the eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden so that only a small extremity attached to its shaft could be found, and it was a large arrow among the sharp arrows of the Turks, with iron lion on which there were no ears.
And the Jew recovered, and was not damaged in his eye despite the severe injury.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which was struck in the shoulder, and it was an Arab arrow, and that one had ears; then I incised over it between two ribs, and it was already submerged in his shoulder; then I separated it finely, and soon extracted it, and the Christian recovered.
And extracted another arrow from a man which was lodged in his belly, and perceived that he died from that; and therefore, after passing thirty days and no change occurring due to any of his dispositions, incised over the arrow and managed it, and he was healed, and did not suffer any ill accident.
And there was a man in whom an arrow struck his back, and the wound had healed over. And after approaching seven years, incise the arrow at the root of the thigh.
And there was a woman to whom an arrow struck her belly, and the wound had healed inside her, and no change occurred in any of her natural operations, and the woman did not experience any discomfort during her operations.
And there was another man to whom an arrow struck his face, and the wound had healed, but broke the arrow, not experiencing any more pain, and appeared similar to this.
And I extracted the arrow from the prince of the king, who was struck in the middle of his nose, and it had slightly tilted to the right side, with the entire arrow hidden.
And I was called for the cure around the wound within three days.
And then you will find the wound of the arrow stretched out; and then you will inquire about the one that feels subtle, and does not feel it; but he felt the puncture when you touched his right ear, and then suspected that this puncture was at the tip of the arrow.
And apply the place to be applied with an attractive and digestive plaster, trusting that the place we apply it will show me the location of the arrow, so it can dry on that area.
And do not miss the significant location where the arrow hit; and continue applying the plaster over it for several days without missing any spot; and meanwhile, the wound consolidates, and the patient experiences pain from the removal of the arrow after a few days, and this happens to me.
And then place a sharp corrosive medicine on the wound for several days until it becomes inflamed, and then inquire about that area, finding the tip of the arrow, which adheres to the shaft.
And then you adjust in the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until I visibly see the tip of the arrow, and pass to my time after four months.
And then, once the wound is enlarged and I can insert the forceps into it, I repeatedly work on that, without using any aid, so that I do not cease to finesse and invent various methods of handling it until one day, by chance, I successfully grasp the object with the forceps in a descenting manner as described at the end of this chapter. Then, I restore the wound.
And some doctors judged that the cartilage of the nose is not restored, but I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the patient recovered completely, with no scar from any cause.
And I recount to you the quality of extracting some arrows, so that you pause and consider this rule and meaning behind what I have not said, for the parts of this art and the definitions of those are not understood in narration nor do they comprehend those freely.
And the wise craftsmen compare little on motion; and on what is present they compare it to what is absent; and find new operations and new instruments for the descent of foreign causes when they occur.
And thus I say that arrows are not extricated from the members in which they are inserted but according to two manners: either by attraction of the place in which they enter; or by the contrary part.
And that which is extricated by the place where it entered, or the arrow penetrating a fleshly part and being inserted and then extricated.
And if it does not obey the impression of the hour in which it entered, then you should leave it for a few days until the flesh around it heals and the attraction and its exit are alleviated.
And similarly, if it is inserted into bone and does not obey your impression, then leave that for a few days and apply attraction and movement to it each day, and it will eventually be extricated.
And if you do not obey your attempt for some days, then you must make a perforation in the circuit of the arrow at that bone of any part moving subtly within which the area is amplified by the arrow.
But if the arrow is already hidden in the place of the body and hidden from sensation, then you inquire about that area with your hand, and if you feel it, then probe with some instruments which are suitable for its extraction.
And if you cannot remove it due to the depth of the wound and the length of the arrow, and here there are neither nerves nor veins, then you cut into the wound to expand it and make it possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is in the ears where it is lodged, then you cut into the flesh in which it is embedded, completely dislodging it with all that you can do; and use your skill here, if you cannot withdraw the flesh in the fracture of the ears and in the torsion of those parts that must obey.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow in any place where it is, then you use your hand twisted with forceps at all parts to make it obey; and with subtle skill, ensure that the arrow is not broken and that it is made easy for its extraction.
And if you cannot operate on that side during your turn, then you let it for some days until the parts that are in the circuit of him heal, and then operate on the other side which is then alleviated. And if it causes you contrary effects from the blood, then you administer what we have said about cure in its chapter.
And preserve your virtue in the incision of veins or nerves, or cords, and administer ease in every manner for the escape of the arrow; and do this with facilitation, tranquility, slowness, and firmness as you advise.
And therefore make the arrow contract by itself so that the patient is according to the figure according to which you should place the arrow in him, for this is more advantageous. And if this is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you according to the figures.
And for arrows that are deflected contrary to the other part, or that you have already treated some part of them from the external parts, or cause congestion in the proximal end of the arrow by the feeling of the part above the femoral pulse; then extract it over that; and let the extraction be according to the quantity in which the forceps take hold; and then remove it, for it is made light with evisceration.
But if it is retained in the joint, then twist the leg according to the opposite direction where the arrow presses on the joint, and enlarge it slightly, and then extract it.
But if not, leave it for a few days, and then repeat the procedure where it is sore.
But if the shaft of the arrow is there, then push it into it.
But if the shaft is embedded and you wish to administer pressure, then insert it there. If it is difficult, stir it gently so that the concavity of the shaft aligns with the channel of the arrow, and then push it into place.
But if the arrow is concave, then push it with strong stirring into it, for the arrow will be expanded by this.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then ensure that you concave all the flesh where it is located, if possible, and then treat it with whatever is appropriate.
And if the arrow hits the foot, or the abdomen, or the bladder, or the side, and is close enough to where you touch it directly, and it is possible to extract it there, then extract it and carefully remove any clots of blood or nerves, and stitch the wound so that it may heal.
Form of forceps with which arrows are extracted:
If their ends are shaped like the beak of a bird, made such that they grasp the arrow when placed on it, so as not to let it slip.
And from these are made special forceps, large, few, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its shape.
So that it may be alleviated for the patient in the concave groove and is pressed towards it.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.

Chapter Two: Of Fractures When They Occur in the Head.
The types of fractures are cracks, and their figures are various, and the causes are distinct. For some fractures, they are caused by the impact of a blow, which breaks the bone completely through the cranium, leaving the bone in such a way as it does in the trunk, and for this reason, this type is named *ascial*; or the blow affects a part of the bone, cutting the flesh but not passing through the marrow; and this type of fracture is named *levatio absoluta*.
And the swelling of these two types of fractures can be great or small, and the frictional fracture is caused by a blow with a rod or stone, resembling them in appearance.
And this fracture can be penetrating, nearing the periosteum which is attached to the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the swelling of these two types of fractures can be great or small.
And this type of fracture is softened in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair, and for this it is called this species of capillary fracture.
And this is a fracture caused by compression or percussion of a stone, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the internal parts, and it occurs in such concave locations as happen to stone quarries when they are struck, with part of their entry into the internal parts.
And this is more commonly caused in the bodies of moist bones, such as those of infants.
And all these species of comminuted fractures are bad like stars separated. And I will return to the recollection of all this in its place.
And we know all these species of fracture through detection by touch and by inquiry about the broken flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary fracture is known because it is discovered on the bone, is extracted, and limits on it by touch, for the fracture appears black.
In the cure of fracture consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and when you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification over fear, such as vomiting of colera, extension, loss of understanding, absence of voice, syncope, acute fever, swelling of the eyes and redness of those, and signs like these in the greater dispositions, suspect that it is not healed.
And if you see uncommon accidents, you hope for health, and then you start the cure of it.
And the cause is that if the injury has come to you in the beginning as injured and this is during the days of winter, then consider that when you study in the bone's membrane before the fourteenth day according to all disposition.
And if it is during the days of summer, then consider that you cut the bone on the seventh day, having that what is left of the bone be corrupted by the fatty tissue, and that the accidents we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the fatty tissue covering the marrow, and is finely divided and bruised, then consider that you incise the fragmented part and bruise it, according to what I have recounted to you.
And you should shave the patient's thigh, and uncover the bone according to custom and your possible method according to the shape of the wound, and do so lightly over the patient.
And if it happens to you to detect the bone, fluxes of blood or abscess caut, then you should apply what fits, and that you fill the area with infused cloths in wine and rubbed oil, and make the wound rest until the abscess is subdued, and you are sure of the fluxes of blood; and then proceed with the circuit of the bone and its ablation.
And do so according to one of two surgical methods.
And one method is to incise the bone with an incisor straight such, and this is its form: And then administer another incisor here, a little more lateral than the first; and this is its form.
And then use more the incisor slightly more lateral than the second.
And ensure that you have the right number of different incisors, some of which are more lateral than others; and some of them are shorter and sharper at their extremities, and made of iron or steel.
And use their assistance in percussion over the incisions to prevent shock and lead them.
And if the bone is hard, first make a perforation in its circuit before you administer the incisions along the tendons, which you will call shallow incisions.
And they are not called so, but rather those that do not reach the end of the bone's length, by which the tendon has a round extremity or margin near it, resembling a small edge or circle. You must prevent this from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you use a large number of tendons, each suitable for the thickness of each bone. While the head of the tendon is sharp at its extremity, the length and shortness of the quantity should be according to the thickness of this bone.
And this is the method for three types of tendons: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is to place the tendon on the bone and rotate it with the needles until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then, move the tendon to another location, and pause where all of it reaches the quantity of the thickness of the ray or around it.
And then incise with an incisor where it is between each tooth of the bone, and make that small incision delicate which you can do at the end of levity, as we have said, during the removal of the bone, or by your hand, or by some other cause through certain instruments which you will prepare for this, such as fine scissors and forceps.
And see that you take all precautions so that the incisor does not touch any part of the cavity.
And thus, after you have circumvented the bone perforation and separated it from the cavity if it is continuous with it, and you extract that part, see that you scrape and level it at the roughness of that which is in the bone with another instrument resembling an incisor, but however see that it be finer and more delicate than other incisors.
And if you remove any small part of the bones and scrape, then take that with ease using one of the instruments; and then cure the wound with ointment and apply the ones we have said.
And the other method of operation is gentle, far from fear, which he called Gualia and cut with great caution; this is his saying: At the beginning, you must necessarily start uncovering the part of the bone in the area where the fracture is most severe and most obvious. Once you have uncovered that part and placed your hand gently and cheerfully at the end of this long incisor from which this takes its form:
And the lens-shaped part is lion and you must not cut any cause, and the acute part is from it into two cuts forward in length, as you see; that the lens-shaped part be adjusted to the chife against the face of the acute incision on the bone.
And then strike upon the incisor of one part with a small hammer as you cut through the entire bone with ease, and you be sure of the panniculus that it does not occur in the accident that you fear in any way, and if the medicant were the most fool of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were asleep.
When there is any additional cause affecting the bone of the ankle in some parts of the bone, it will incise that one at the extremity of the incised lenticular, and facilitate that one, for it is delivered from its damage and fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the ankle, and what is excised or remains of the bone is its surface, and it is rough and subtle irregularities in it, then you must razors that roughness and eradicate those subtle irregularities with your prepared sharp razors, in various quantities so that you can administer to all those places the most convenient parts of them and the right part according to where you have directed your operation on that broken bone and its figure.
And you must administer with your judgment using the razors to start shaving the bone with the coarser part of those razors, and then use the finer part of them, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the coarse and fine parts of all.
In any other small capillary fracture or subtle fracture, you must administer care according to where you have directed for the correction of it.
And it is because they are not accustomed to treating all those who have studied that art. And place this on what we have written about in great fractures.
And if the gauze is torn when you extract the bone, then take a piece of cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in olive oil, placing it over the wound's opening; then take another piece of folded or triple-folded cloth and immerse it in wine and olive oil, placing it over the first piece. Do this gently enough so as not to irritate the tissue; then apply linseed poultice over it, but do not press it more than the size of the wound allows.
And leave it to this treatment for one day or two until you are sure of no abscess; then remove it and administer medicines that require additional dryness, such as linseed and wheat flour, and powder of thyme and arsenic, and similar substances.
And you make these medicines into a powder that is sprinkled on the wound, as it is dry; generally, administer to it any medicine that has the property of cleansing and not irritate.
And guard thou according to thy virtue that the wound in thy cure be clean; and not therein surdician of ointment nor other; nor layest thou to add virulence therein; for when it is adjusted upon the linen of the brain, corrupt that and mar that, and happeneth upon the patient near this malign evil.
And it happens in the scab of the head, as from it is modified the bone and most especially when the cure of it is neglected, and blackness appears on the surface thereof; and therefore consider when it happens to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious indeed.
And if no blackness accideth but from the medicine that is applied thereon, and it is in virtue of that medicine which causeth that blackness, then take that thou mixest a part of honey with three parts of rubbed oil, and line those lintels with a cloth, and place it over the scab; and cure it with the spices of cure that suit until it be healed.
Capitol iii.
For the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not the nose itself that is broken, but two parts joined by an cartilaginous piece, or one of those few blows, because in the nose there are two bones. The part that breaks is cartilaginous and you do not injure it, but rather through friction, tortuosity, and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts is broken, you must introduce the small bone into the nasal cavity, and equalize the fracture inside according to your indicated index, and externally, between the foot and the nose, so that the nose returns to its natural figure. And do this for you with facilitation, and guard that the pain in the patient does not worsen due to your operation.
And if the fracture is in the cartilaginous part of the nose, and the bone does not reach there, you must equalize it at the extremity of the ray where there is a small swelling.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you make the appearance of this.
And if the swelling has a restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; otherwise, then return the inflammation to its place with a pointed cautery.
And then insert into the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or two walnuts, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the walnut swell to such an extent that it fills the nostril.
And say these ancient restorers that the walnuts should be wrapped in cloth, and changed throughout the day. But this is not seen by me; instead, have a cavity where the piece of cloth is wrapped in ox gall mixed with powdered spice; and leave the walnut until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes insert into the nose instead of a walnut, a hot piece wrapped in lion's cloth; and ensure that the fracture of the nose is very severe and that the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, do others.
And if another abscess occurs, cauterize it, plug the nose with wool or a wet and rubbed fat cloth, or with a pledget of oil.
But if no abscess occurs, when you need to plug it externally, use sesame seed powder and powdery coptis root with honey plaster; then place your hand on the nose to hold it in place, and do not plug the nose for any cause.
But if the nose bones are fractured or reduced, when you need to split those areas, extract them using instruments suitable for those areas; then, at the site of the split, treat it with what constricts and consolidates, and use appropriate plasters.
And if a swelling occurs inside the nose, when you need to treat it, do so with poultices and administer lead pipes internally until it is completely healed.
Capitol. iv.
For the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.

And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you must administer extension to both parts according to the straight line within which it is possible to align them.
And if you encounter movement of teeth with disruption, then strengthen those that you expect to break at the edge of gold or silver or copper; and place a cautery on the broken jaw, and place one on the doubled cloth, and another on the large star-shaped piece made properly, or a single piece equal in length to the jaw; and place this above as appropriate for your ligature and application, so long as it is not destroyed.

Chapter V.
On the restoration of the furcula when it is fractured.
When the scapula is fractured, in the anterior part you should break the muscle, and that fracture can be made in one of three ways.
When it is fractured, or separated, or separated into two parts without undergoing stages; and the restoration is easier for such; or if it happens to undergo stages, and the restoration is difficult; or thus that the fracture occurs in the wound.
And the operation in this case, when the fracture is in the wound, is that you have two ministers present, so that one holds the assistant and follows the fractured scapula, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And then equalize the fracture by placing it in the groins as it is made according to its form, and do not place it in a slight prominence or depression.
And if you need more extension, when you place the patient's limb on a pillow or cloth, let its length be according to your necessity; extend and elevate the femur and compress the pillow with your hand as you equalize the fracture according to its form.
And if you cannot extend the end of the femur to the outer parts because it is stuck at the hip, then place the patient supine on their back, and rest a middle-sized cushion on the muscle.
And the minister presses on the muscle at the outer parts until the bone of the femur is elevated to the hip joint, and then you correct the fracture and equalize it in the groins.
But if you feel the bones of the femur and they are fragmented, then cut there and remove those fragments easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you skillfully incise it with one of the incisors which you have prepared for this, after placing another servant to hold the instrument that serves the ciphac; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is its form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is its cavity; and its width depends on what you need of it, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible in the operation; that it have two extremities thus like spoons, and one extremity broad and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you have extracted the fragment from the bone is large, and there is a certain abscess cavity, then you adjust the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear an abscess cavity, then you manage the wound with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the incision of the wound.
And if you encounter an abscess, then inject wet cloth or rosin-soaked cloth into it and place something over it.
And always ensure that the stick does not touch the broken bone; therefore, you should check the wound daily, as when the bandage becomes softened or you see the stick already loosened, then you adjust it and strengthen the binding.
And then place the patient's head on its pillow, and let it rest in such a way that a cushion is under its neck; thus, the support of the affected side is raised, and therefore the fracture of the furcula is elevated to the elevation of the muscle; or the arm of the patient is laid along the side of this.
And do not remove the bandage unless something happens at the site or an abscess develops within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see any signs of infection at the site, and adjust it to fit properly and restore the area, allowing the fracture of the furcula to heal and coagulate.
And it is strengthened and made comfortable according to the condition over twenty-eight days, and in some cases, it is done in less time than that.

Capitol VII.
On the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes breaks in the middle of its location and remains unhealed for some time; more often, its ends become swollen and necrotic.
And of the accidents when the foot breaks in its middle, it tends to decline towards the internal parts and causes great pain, difficulty in walking, coughing, fever, and vomiting; these are evident in the cavity of the broken bone and are not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration is that he be laid on his back, and a cushion placed between his shoulders; then press on his muscles, adjust his spine against the vertebrae, and carefully perform the operation and adjustment in every way possible until the figure of the bone returns as it should.
And then place it on the plaster and sponge, and lay it over the table with a thin layer of wax or alum and something similar in levitation, so that you wrap that in cloth; then carefully apply it to the broken bone without pressing too much; and cover it with bandages according to its roundness on the back several times, and strengthen it with proper tension; and always consider the bandage at all times, and when it is softened, strengthen it.
And if necessity causes an abscess, inflammation, or pain to occur in that area, when you incise and lance it, remove the plaster and correct what occurs by this method of healing; then reduce the plaster, if you see fit; and adjust its tension as it heals.
Chapter VIII.
For the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.
Know that a fracture of the ribs occurs only in large areas where the two ends meet; and from the extremities of the front part, such contact does not cause fractures because they are cartilaginous. And the perception of it is not hidden to the sense of touch by placing your hand on it.
And the restoration should be done by equalizing the fracture with your hand as much as possible, ensuring it fits naturally, and then apply a plaster to strengthen the broken bone, if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs is near the internal parts, it commonly causes severe pain and puncture, similar to the pain that occurs when there is pleurisy. For this reason, one places a bandage on the bone; and it leads to difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting due to blood. This is a difficult condition to cure.
And already the Ancients were ingenious in such matters.
And from them we say: place the patient in a position where foods that cause inflammation and swelling are avoided, so that the belly may swell and be extended, and the fracture may be brought to the outward parts.
But we advise this so that it may not be a trivial cause for generating a carbuncle accidentally; and if one does occur, then aid is already at hand.
And some of them said that it should be placed above the belly and near where sweat flows strongly; and this seems logical, but it is feared that the belly might drive the superfluities to the area due to its weakness.
And some of them said: place the area covered with wool that has been submerged in oil, and let it be wrapped with powder between the ribs until it is full, ensuring the dressing remains equal as you wrap it according to roundness; and then treat the patient with a regimen of food cure and medicine.
And if the patient is strongly constrained and does not suffer, and the skin is punctured with a needle for drainage, be careful about the patient, then cut open at that place, uncovering from the broken side, and place ourselves over that instrument which we have retained, the memory of which precedes, and incise the skin easily, and extract it.
And when we adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suturing, and cure that with plasters until it is healed.
And if you happen to find an abscess inside, then incise and insert powder in olive oil and place over the area, and cure the patient with what is appropriate for the abscess inside.
And let it be dressed over the cloth on which he sleeps until it is healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae in the back and neck.
In some cases, when fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and tendons are also injured, similar to the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be cured or not, then consider whether their pulse is already softened, numbed, and dead and cannot increase or extend or strengthen those pulses and when you constrain them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and do not feel pain in them, then you know that it is not curable, and in such cases, it is fatal.
And if you feel pain in them when you constrain them with your fingers and prick them with a needle, then you know that the nerve of the bone is saved and that gout will heal by cure.
And if you feel along the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if it will be curable or not, then consider the pulse in his feet.
And if you feel that they are already softened, apply yourself to what happens in the pulse, and when he lies on his back, breathe on him and induce vomiting at his will, and sometimes lift him on his back and want to urinate but cannot, then you know it is fatal, and then do not trouble with his cure.
But if no other cause occurs, the cause is better and lighter.
And the cure for this is to perforate with a needle at the site where you place it over the lumbar or slightly scraped spine, or with hot irons; place it there for several minutes each day until the abscess is numbed.
And then place one of the comforting and healing plasters, and apply it firmly over that area; command the patient to take rest and be tranquil, and not to sleep on the part where he feels pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that you need to scrape the bone surface or any cause that is detached from it, then clean the wound and keep the bone in place; then close the two edges of the wound,缝合如果伤口较大；然后用一些膏药治疗该部位，直到痊愈。

And if you feel broken phases in it, then operate on that, stop it, and cure the wound with a cure that is to precede until it is healed.

Capitol.xi.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the adjutrix.
Adjutori is that which is between the codium (groin) and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, the restoration of it is in one of two manners, of which one is that you take a rod of good wood according to this form:
And let two threads be at the ends, and then suspend them from the elevated area.
And then place the patient on a bench; and then throw its broken arm onto the rod so that it reaches the middle point where the curvature of the rod is attached.

And the other method is that the patient be resuspended over its own neck and suspend the hand of its collar with a thread; and then command two servants that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the rest below it; and each of them extend to their part.

And so if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then place the middle of the stick over the tendon and let the other end rest on the fracture to reduce it.
And similarly if the fracture is near the hip, then place the stick over that location and the hip.
Equalize that with ease and your work, and then strengthen it as soon as it is equalized according to what is needed and applied properly.

And then use splints.

And ensure that the joint around the location of the fracture is stronger. And when the fracture is longer, make the joint less tight.

If you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not loosen the ligature for at least three days or more.
Let the patient lie on their back, and their hands be over their stomach.
Place yourself over a warm pad filled with wool, and at all times during the night or day, consider whether the figure of the broken member remains intact, or if the ligament has become softened; therefore, correct this entirely with your virtue.
Feed the patient according to the order we have mentioned before, ensuring that the food is subtle at first until it begins to strengthen; then gradually increase the quantity of the food.
By custom, the bandage and belt are tightened for ten days; then they should be loosened and a bath should be administered, along with ointments that will correct this.
And if the fracture has been irritated, then do not remove the lint or stitches within one or two months.
Capitol. xii.
On the restoration of fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the humerus; of which the one is small and is that which we call the upper arm, and the other is large and is that which is placed above the smaller one.
And if the large bone of the humerus is broken only, or the small one, and if they are both broken together.
And when the small bone is broken above, then the restoration of it is light, and the healing of it is more light.
And when the large bone is broken below, then the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the broken bone is the small thigh bone above, then it is convenient for the doctor to perform the restoration of it, which involves a slight extension facilitated between when that area is stabilized.
But if the large thigh bone is what is broken, then it is necessary to perform a more vigorous extension.
And if both thigh bones are broken together, then you must perform the reduction more forcefully.
And you must place your hand figure to facilitate the restoration and extension of the bone lying on a cushion, with the hand wrist above, more so than the other fingers, and the little finger be below the other fingers, and the thigh be centered on a sloping board.
And place a cushion in front of it at its length or upright position, so that the thigh does not come to bear any weight.
And then the minister extend the arm of the injured part, placing it on his own hand or a cushion, and another minister extend it similarly above.
And then the doctor straightens the bone back into place according to the best form that can be achieved.
And if in the fracture of the bone you have successes, then you reduce all pieces into their proper place with your virtue.
And if there appears a separate piece that cannot be reduced to its proper place as you hope for in the restoration of it, then you set about it and reduce that according to the manner which we have said above.
But if the fracture is open, I have already treated this in a separate chapter, and therefore take up the treatment of it.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you line a cloth made of cotton soaked in olive oil and white wax, making it medium in thickness and fineness, and tightly wrap the cloth around that with a subtle constraint through which the abscess can drain; then remove the wax and apply a prepared plaster of powder of mustard in honey, and place a bandage over it; and let the bandage be placed above that fracture on the side and more firmly.
And you shall know that the number of bones in the arm is seen in the major dispositions, or rather in a fracture in one bone or two bones together.
And then place your grip firmly and strongly over the location of the fracture. When the tension is greatest either at the upper parts of the fracture or at the lower parts of it, place the tension a little more mild according to how the memory of it is recalled at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be moistened cloths, neither hard nor rough. The linen which is strengthened should be made of medium-fine material, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the ligament for several days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, as is often the case with the limb, then you should immerse the limb in water until that disturbance ceases.
And let the limb not be bound until it has rested, and then return the grip.
And if the constriction has already been softened and the bone has already been restored and appears as those, then correct all of this with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from reaching the member due to excessive constriction, so that you soften it a little and leave it for some days until the nourishment flows to that area, and then strengthen it.
And if to the patient none of the causes we have said occurs, then you do not need to let it slip for about 20 days or around those.
And place the patient's hand for its virtue of movement agitation, and have them sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or in 32 days, or more fully restored in 28 days, and all of this depends on the complexity and the workings of virtue.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of fractures of the thumb and fingers.
Know that at the wrist of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers touch gently and do not damage them but rub with seen threads.

Chapter XIV.
For the restoration of fracture of the hip bone.

Capitol.xxiii.
On the speech concerning dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of that location, such that movement is prohibited, and the figure of the limb is distorted, causing great pain and injuries in the patient.

Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the jaw, proceed.
